    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="a004371d24c04a958b54e3ae9e16f278">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '629a4f7c-6083-489d-a735-0303678597da'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d68468183" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Different dimensions of carbon materials with various features have captured numerous interests due to their applications on the tremendous fields. Restricted by the raw materials and devices, the controlling of their morphology is a major challenge. Utilizing the catalytic features of the intermediates from the low-cost salts and polymerization of 0D carbon quantum dots (CQDs), 0D CQDs are expected to self-assemble into 1/2/3D carbon structures with the assistance of temperature-induced intermediates (e.g., ZnO, Ni, and Cu) from the salts (ZnCl<sub>2</sub>, NiCl<sub>2</sub>, and CuCl). The formation mechanisms are illustrated as follows: 1) the “orient induction” to evoke “vine style” growth mechanism of ZnO; 2) the “dissolution–precipitation” of Ni; and 3) the “surface adsorption self-limited” of Cu. Subsequently, the degree of graphitization, interlayer distance, and special surface area are investigated in detail. 1D structure from 700 °C as anode displays a high Na-storage capacity of 301.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles and 107 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 5000 cycles. Quantitative kinetics analysis confirms the fundamentals of the enhanced rate capacity and the potential region of Na-insertion/extraction. This elaborate work opens up an avenue toward the design of carbon with multidimensions and in-depth understanding of their sodium-storage features.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs595-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs595-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Carbon, as most closely related to human, has been distributed spaciously in the nature, and then spontaneously devoted to enormous attentions in physical and chemical properties. Interestingly, various atomic hybrid orbitals were observed, including sp, sp<sup>2</sup>, and sp<sup>3</sup> characteristics.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0001_R_d68468175e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater. Mater. Today Nano Today Adv. Mater.">1</a></span> Moreover, the anisotropy of crystal and other arrangements are dominated by that of sp<sup>2</sup>, giving rise to the innumerable characteristics. In fact, carbon is unique among total elements as the single unit to form 0D fullerenes,<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0002_R_d68468175e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Science">2</a></span> 1D carbin and nanotube,<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0003_R_d68468175e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Water Res. ACS Nano">3</a></span> 2D graphene sheets and 3D diamond crystal,<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0004_R_d68468175e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Materials J. Materiomics Nano–Micro Lett.">4</a></span> which are utilized in a myriad of applications, such as biosensors, catalyst, renewable energy storage, and so on.[<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0004_R_d68468175e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Materials J. Materiomics Nano–Micro Lett.">4</a></span>] In addition, allotropes of carbon have various properties, from hard (diamond) to soft (graphite), from insulative (diamond) to semiconductive (graphite) and conductive (graphene), and from light absorbing (graphite) to diaphanous (diamond).<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0005_R_d68468175e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Mater. Sci. Eng., C">5</a></span> Considering its fascinating potential, numerous efforts were further made for carbon to explore different dimensions, and great progress was achieved as expected.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0006_R_d68468175e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Inspiringly, 0D carbon quantum dots (CQDs) are found,<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0007_R_d68468175e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">7</a></span> enclosing (1) the graphite CQDs came from the top-down manner based on the target materials, containing carbon fiber, oxidized graphene, and carbonated organic products resulted from high temperature, which exhibit the lamellar structure and good crystallinity;[<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0007_R_d68468175e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">7</a>, <a href="#advs595-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0008_R_d68468175e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Alloys Compd.">8</a></span>] (2) the polymerized CQDs derived from small organic molecules through the down-top process (e.g., carbohydrate, citric acid, and amino acid), with abundant functional groups and weak crystallinity.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0009_R_d68468175e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Sci. Chem. Mater. Biosens. Bioelectron.">9</a></span> Note that extreme conditions or special devices are necessary in most of the fabrication methods, seriously restraining the advancements of CQDs. Recently, facile and effective organic CQDs are prepared with extensive organic functional groups, which have been successfully applied in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) by transferring themselves to 3D structures.[<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0009_R_d68468175e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Sci. Chem. Mater. Biosens. Bioelectron.">9</a></span>] With this evolution, the carbon materials would have large changes in their properties. For 1D carbon structures, it can be summarized to be of two main kinds: (1) carbon nanotubes (CNTs) mainly with sp-hybridized carbon atoms, containing single-walled and multiwalled carbon nanotubes, are deemed with wrapping graphite layers and perfect tubes in structure, showing the high modulus, strength, and conductivity;<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0010_R_d68468175e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Soc. Rev.">10</a></span> (2) carbon fibers (CNFs) mostly with sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon structure, displaying the nonsequence monofilaments with the length/diameter ratios larger than 100 times.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0011_R_d68468175e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a></span> As well known, graphene is the hot topic among 2D carbon structures, including graphene oxides (GOs) and reduced graphene oxides, which are composed of the linking of carbon's six-atom rings to form a 2D honeycomb network.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0012_R_d68468175e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Mater. Today Chem. Soc. Rev.">12</a></span> Surprisingly, treated with high energy consumption, graphene can be employed to buckle the spherical structures (0D fullerenes), 1D CNTs, and layered structures (3D graphite). The state-of-the-art graphene has also been devoted to extensive attentions with its great physicochemical properties, such as outstanding electrical, thermal conductivity, and high specific surface area.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0013_R_d68468175e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature Nature">13</a></span> As the highest dimensional carbon nanostructures, 3D structure is comprised of 0D CQDs to form bulk carbon, 1D CNFs to present the network, 2D graphene to fabricate microporous structures, which would effectively prevent the fundamental units from agglomerating, showing the stable frame structure.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0014_R_d68468175e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Sustainable Energy Fuels J. Mater. Chem. A Nanoscale">14</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Carbon structures with various dimensions are obtained traditionally through unique strategies, which are seriously limited by raw materials and particular equipment. For instances, the electrospinning process was applied to fabricate 1D polymer nanofibers, which could be further carbonized to produce CNFs.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0015_R_d68468175e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Chem. A Small">15</a></span> Micromechanical exfoliation as the primitive approach is still grateful to effectively prepare 2D graphene at present.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0016_R_d68468175e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Acc. Chem. Res. Energy Environ. Sci.">16</a></span> Although the catalytic chemical vapor deposition can be used to fabricate 1D and 2D carbon materials, the required devices and catalytic nanoparticles are essential, where iron is used to constitute the CNFs and copper is employed to assemble 2D graphene.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0017_R_d68468175e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Chem. Eng. J. Small">17</a></span> Certainly, the whole dimensions of carbon nanostructures can also be obtained by the biomasses,<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0018_R_d68468175e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ChemPhotoChem Nano J. Solid State Electrochem. Appl. Surf. Sci. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></span> but the raw materials are restricted. The bee pollen, cellulose, corn, and peat mosses are transformed into 0D CQDs, 1D CNFs, 2D graphene, as well as 3D macroscopic carbon frameworks, respectively. To date, controlling carbon morphologies is still unsolved. As motivated by the Antonietti and co-workers' report,<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0019_R_d68468175e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">19</a></span> salts play an important role in the carbon target through the function of molten salt, which is ascribed that the salts would display numerous chemical and physical transformations with the raised temperature. Meanwhile, compared with the aforementioned manners, the controllable salt methods are experienced a renaissance with their favorable features, showing that it will be expected for an ideal technique to diverse carbon nanostructures more effectively.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, for the first time, the CQDs are successfully evolved into 1D-wrapped CNF, 2D large-scale carbon nanosheets (CNSs), and 3D porous carbon framework (CFW). The corresponding formation mechanisms of those samples are investigated in details, showing that (1) the “orient induction” of ZnO, (2) the “dissolution–precipitation” of Ni, and (3) the “surface adsorption self-limited” of Cu. For 1D structures, the vines' style growth mechanism was triggered. Such evolutions lead to varied electrochemical performances of the formed carbon structures. Most greatly, 1D CNFs display high reversible capacities of 301.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 107 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 5000 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs595-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs595-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs595-sec-0030">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs595-sec-0030-title">2.1 The Evolution form 0D CQDs to 1/2/3D Carbon Structure</h3>
            
            <p>The effects of different salts on the microstructure of the as-prepared samples were systematically investigated. The detailed morphologies and crystal structures of 1D CNF, 2D CNS, and 3D CFW were revealed by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs595-fig-0001">1</a>. The images of 0D CQDs with different magnification are shown in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0001">1</a>A, which were quite uniform and monodispersed with the diameters in the range of 3.0–5.0 nm. Without the introduction of any salts, 0D CQDs were instantly transformed into bulk carbon particles at 700 °C as shown in Figure 1S (Supporting Information). Impacted by ZnCl<sub>2</sub>, NiCl<sub>2</sub>, and CuCl, 0D CQDs were manipulated to 1D CNF, 2D CNS, and 3D CFW, respectively. Note that 1D CNF was formed through the wrapping of the small CNFs in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0001">1</a>B, differing from that of the previous report.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0011_R_d68468175e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a></span> Moreover, the large-scale sheet of 2D CNS is found, and the porous framework of 3D CFW is presented in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0001">1</a>C,D. These interesting evolutions demonstrate that multidimensional carbon structures are surely shaped through different features of salts, which is worthy to explore their corresponding formation mechanisms. With the careful observation, it is found that numerous defects existed on the surface of CNFs display shuttle shapes, which were wrapped from small primary CNFs with the diameter of ≈80 nm in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0001">1</a>B. Clearly, 2D-inflected sheets are composed of some thin sheets, and there are plenty of microspores on the surface of 3D CFW structures in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0001">1</a>C,D. As shown in the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0001">1</a>B–D, the graphitic lattice spacing of 1D CNF, 2D CNS, and 3D CFW is ≈0.39 nm, ≈0.38 nm, ≈0.35 nm, respectively. Significantly, all of them would be beneficial for metal ions (e.g., Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>) insertion/desertion, resulted from the relatively larger graphite lattice than that of graphite (0.335 nm).<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0020_R_d68468175e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Commun.">20</a></span> Moreover, the number of layer-stacked-graphene sheets decreases in sequence: 1D CNF &gt; 2D CNS &gt; 3D CFW, which is further confirmed through the following X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs595-fig-0002">2</a>. The reason of the observed diverse lattice distances is deduced that the van der Waals' forces of tiny sheets in the microcrystalline graphite can be altered by the salts in the carbonization process.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0021_R_d68468175e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Arch. Appl. Mech. Qual. Theory Dyn. Syst.">21</a></span> In addition, note that the brightness of the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) images is improved in the order of 1D CNF &lt; 2D CNS &lt; 3D CFW, showing the increased degree of graphite. Interestingly, the elliptical diffraction ring and long-term disordered lattice of 1D CNF are noticed, demonstrating that the preferred orientation is displayed by the growth of carbon atoms.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs595-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/64351180-9f59-4870-9ae4-9a691272de37/advs595-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/64351180-9f59-4870-9ae4-9a691272de37/advs595-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/75eaf76a-c98f-4edb-aecc-70823a7416c3/advs595-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/64351180-9f59-4870-9ae4-9a691272de37/advs595-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs595-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201800080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM and HRTEM images of A1–A3) 0D CQDs, B1–B3) 1D CNF, C1–C3) 2D CNS, and D1–D3) 3D CFW.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs595-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b5e7a14b-8c12-470a-9962-b3b0bb5280c4/advs595-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b5e7a14b-8c12-470a-9962-b3b0bb5280c4/advs595-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1fcd5c3f-03c6-4adf-8739-29d0d799d4b7/advs595-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b5e7a14b-8c12-470a-9962-b3b0bb5280c4/advs595-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs595-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201800080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images for A–C) 1D CNF, D–F) 2D CNS, G–I) and 3D CFW.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The large-scale morphology of the as-obtained samples was also investigated through scanning electron microscopy (SEM). Without salts, CQDs are carbonized to irregular bulk carbon in Figure 1S (Supporting Information). The uniformly distributed CNFs with the length of 2–8 µm are obtained as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs595-fig-0003">3</a>A, suggesting that the ZnCl<sub>2</sub> molten salt (melting point, 290 °C) provides a stable reaction matrix and the in situ formed ZnO brings adequate active sites to favor the formation of 1D structure. As depicted in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0003">3</a>B,C, a mean diameter of shuttle shape CNF with rough surface is about 150 nm, indicating that numerous defects were existed in CNF, which can facilitate the insertion–extraction of sodium ions. Meanwhile, owing to the catalysis functions of the Ni plate from the NiCl<sub>2</sub>, the large-area CNS is observed in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0003">3</a>D, presenting the bending and uneven characteristics. In Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0003">3</a>E, the carbon sheets consist of 3–5 monolayer carbon papers with harsh surface. When 0D CQDs were assembled into 3D CNF, the tremendous framework was produced with bubbles' structure as shown in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0003">3</a>G. To examine the surface carefully, many pores with diameters of 200 nm are found, in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0003">3</a>G–I, coming from the removal of large Cu particles formed by the reduction of CuCl with carbon. The corresponding mechanisms were further vitrified through the intermediate productions in Figure 2S (Supporting Information). It is proved that the catalysts of multidimensional carbon structure are ZnO, Ni, and Cu, leading to the evolution of internal structures. Compared to the morphology of salts in Figure 3S (Supporting Information), it is noteworthy that the self-generated intermediate products (ZnO, Ni, and Cu) are crucial for the self-assembly of carbon atoms to 1/2/3D structures.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs595-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/09162c77-95fd-4902-a973-153c3814ef9e/advs595-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/09162c77-95fd-4902-a973-153c3814ef9e/advs595-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/59e6fc6a-939f-4082-ac3f-55d673d86dca/advs595-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/09162c77-95fd-4902-a973-153c3814ef9e/advs595-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs595-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201800080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A) X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns, B) Raman spectroscopy, C) nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherm, and D) X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) of 1D CNF, 2D CNS, and 3D CFW.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Obviously, with the introduction of carbon sources at the high temperature (700 °C), the chemical and physical properties of salts would have an enormous transformation: (1) ZnCl<sub>2</sub> was turned into fluid accompanied with ZnO to supply stable matrix; and (2) NiCl<sub>2</sub> and CuCl were chemically reduced to metal substances by the intermediately produced carbon as demonstrated in Figure 2S (Supporting Information). It is clear that the crystallographic structures of the carbon target were regulated by diverse “calcination partners,” which were clarified by XRD as shown in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0002">2</a>A. Broad (002) and weak (100) diffraction peaks located at ≈23<sup>o</sup> and ≈44<sup>o</sup> are observed, which are classical for the turbostratic structure from the pyrolysis of high polymers due to the crosslinking features of precursors and their unique chemical structure.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0022_R_d68468175e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Lett.">22</a></span> Note that the crystalline nature of 1D CNF was better as compared to 2D CNS and 3D CFW, which was deduced to the following reasons: (1) considering heat conductivity coefficient, that of liquid is always higher than that of gas, suggesting that ZnCl<sub>2</sub> molten salt plays significant roles in favoring the heat transfer; (2) benefitting from ZnCl<sub>2</sub> as a dehydrator and the abundant oxygen of CQDs, the nascent oxygen triggers a cascading effect, contributing to the disentanglement arrangement of carbon atoms, which agrees well with the previous reports.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0023_R_d68468175e688" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - RSC Adv. Nano Energy">23</a></span> Moreover, the shifting of (002) peaks toward lower angle is observed in the order of 1D CNF (22.3°) &lt; 2D CNS (23.1°) &lt; 3D CFW (25.2°). Based on the Bragg's equation, the lattice spaces (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub>) are estimated to be 1D CNF (0.397 nm) &gt; 2D CNS (0.385 nm) &gt; 3D CFW (0.356 nm), all of which are larger than that of the model carbon (i.e., graphite, 0.335 nm), favoring the insertion of metallic ions (e.g., Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>) in the context.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0024_R_d68468175e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Eng. J. Carbon">24</a></span> Certainly, there is some microcrystalline graphite in the disordered carbon, formed by the layer-stacked-graphene sheets. Utilizing the scatter' equation and the full width at half-maximum of (002) from the peaks finding reports, the <i>c</i>-axis length (<i>L<sub>c</sub></i>) can be calculated for 1D CNF (1.45 nm), 2D CNS (0.792 nm), 3D CFW (0.398 nm), representing that the as-obtained carbonized samples are consisted of 5–6 sheets (2.35/0.397 = 5.9), 4–5 sheets (1.88/0.385 = 4.8), and 4–5 sheets (1.56/0.356 = 5.3), as discussed in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#advs595-tbl-0001">1</a>. For 2D CNS, the analysis of atomic force microscopy (AFM) displayed the thickness about 0.178 nm (Figure 17S, Supporting Information). More layer-stacked-graphene sheets are concluded to this reason: the abundant aldehyde (<span class="icomoon"></span>CHO) and carbonyl (<span class="icomoon"></span>CO) groups existed CQDs coming from acetone, and the molten ZnCl<sub>2</sub> as the coupling reagent facilitate the aldol reaction on the surface of active sites, which are faster than the formation of 2D CNS and 3D CFW, giving rise to more generated carbon sheets. Thus, the oxygen-functional groups of the as-derived samples were explored as shown in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0002">2</a>D and Figure 5S (Supporting Information). Obviously, the oxygen contents of the samples are increased in order of 1D CNF (7.37%) &lt; 2D CNS (19.2%) &lt; 3D CFW (33.6%), revealing the catalytic advantages of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> in the aldol reaction. From the Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) analysis of Figure 5S (Supporting Information), it is found that the residual groups of 0D CQDS are mainly C<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>C, C<span class="icomoon"></span>OH, and CO.</p>

               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>In order to further explore the effect of salts on the construction and extent of disordered and graphitic structure of the carbonized samples, Raman spectroscopy was conducted as displayed in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0002">2</a>B. Two separate characteristic peaks of the as-derived samples are exhibited, where D is related to the disordered lattice or defects in the conjugated π system (sp<sup>3</sup>-type carbon), and G is associated with the crystalline graphite (sp<sup>2</sup>-type carbon).[<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0014_R_d68468175e965" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Sustainable Energy Fuels J. Mater. Chem. A Nanoscale">14</a>, <a href="#advs595-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0025_R_d68468175e965" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">25</a></span>] For 1D CNF, the half width at half maximum of G and D bands is larger than those of 2D CNS and 3D CFW, and the right shift of peaks is also observed, suggesting the less content of ordered hexagonal structures.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0026_R_d68468175e968" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></span> Note that Ni or Cu has a catalytic action in favoring the better graphene structure.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0027_R_d68468175e971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Carbon ACS Nano Carbon">27</a></span> From the low fitted <i>A</i><sub>sp3</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>sp2</sub> value (the integrated area of sp<sup>3</sup> to sp<sup>2</sup>) in Figure 5S (Supporting Information), the relative ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> is about 1.15, 0.83, or 0.58 in Table 1S (Supporting Information), respectively. Greatly, the enlarged graphitized carbon in the order of 1D CNF &lt; 2D CNS &lt; 3D CFW is found, which can be attributed to the fact that the graphitic of lattice for 1D CNF possesses more defects as compared to those of other samples, thus enhancing the Na-storage properties.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0028_R_d68468175e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Sci. Small">28</a></span> Considering the result of <i>L<sub>c</sub></i>,<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0022_R_d68468175e996" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Lett.">22</a></span> nanosized voids in the hard carbons are deduced to be created in the spaces of graphite microstructure. The specific surface area and pore distribution were also determined, displaying 173.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for 1D CNF, 338.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for 2D CNS, and 429.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for 3D CFW, which are reasonable for different dimensional structures, and 3D structure supplies the largest space as compared to other samples. In addition, 1D CNF possesses the biggest microspores with a mean diameter of 3.43 nm as well as the largest pores volume in Figure 5S (Supporting Information), which is well consistent with the analysis of Raman, resulted from the activation of ZnCl<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0029_R_d68468175e1014" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Mater. Chem. A RSC Adv. Adv. Mater.">29</a></span></p>
            
            <p>Stimulated by this interesting evolution of microstructure, the corresponding mechanisms are proposed for the effect of salts on morphology in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#advs595-fig-0010">1</a>. As shown by the previous report,<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0030_R_d68468175e1023" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Carbon">30</a></span> numerous align active sites existed on the surface of ZnO, which could promote the development of carbon atoms. Through the transformation of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> to ZnO above 400 °C and the plentiful functional groups (CO, C<span class="icomoon"></span>OH) of 0D CQDs, as confirmed in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0005">5</a> and Figure 5S (Supporting Information), <a href="#advs595-fig-0001">1</a>D CNF was expected to be formed. It is clear that the features of 0D CQDs with good dispersibility are stable as displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs595-fig-0004">4</a> with a low temperature (300 °C). When the mixture of 0D CQDs and ZnCl<sub>2</sub> was bought above 400 °C, 0D CQDs were pyrolyzed to carbon atoms, which were assembled into rods, further being wrapped into 1D shuttle structure. The more detailed mechanism is exhibited in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#advs595-fig-0011">2</a>. Notably, the replacement of NiCl<sub>2</sub> has enormously significant effects on the microstructure to transform 0D CQDs to 2D CNS. Due to the presence of carbon atoms and the high melting point of NiCl<sub>2</sub> (1001 °C), bulk Ni particles with large flat surface were produced through the reduction of NiCl<sub>2</sub>, which is ascribed to NiCl<sub>2</sub> with flat morphology as a template (Figure 3S (Supporting Information)). Interestingly, the carbon atoms were dissolved in the Ni atoms at high temperature, which could result in the precipitation of carbon atoms on the surface of Ni materials, finally forming the large-scale nanosheets.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0031_R_d68468175e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Catal.">31</a></span> However, the multilayer sheets were shown in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0003">3</a>, resulted from the uncontrollable velocity of the extraction for carbon atoms. In comparison with the large NiCl<sub>2</sub> particles, the small particles of CuCl were displayed in Figure 3S (Supporting Information), which could be wrapped by the colloid CQDs. In addition, the cubic Cu with the diameter of 500 nm in Figure 2S (Supporting Information) was reduced from the CuCl, derived from the unique characteristic of CuCl, which is used as a catalyst and a pore-foaming agent. The carbon film was formed around the Cu templates, further connecting to the porous framework. Moreover, the solubility of carbon atoms in Cu is much smaller than that of Ni;<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0031_R_d68468175e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Catal.">31</a></span> its growth mechanism was deemed as surface adsorption self-limited, that is, the catalytic ability of Cu was shielded by the formed structure, which could facilitate the formation of the thin carbon film. With the removal of Cu, the big pores were finally produced as shown in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0003">3</a>. Note that the catalysts in the reaction are unstable, and it is due to the fact that the differences in temperature play an important role in obtaining the target materials. With the rapid increase of temperature, ZnO, Ni, and Cu are formed gradually, and some CQDS were attached to their surface. When the temperature reached 700 °C, the catalysts with activity have been obtained, which can effectively induce the evolution of 0D CQDs.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0032_R_d68468175e1064" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Sci. China Mater. Nat. Commun.">32</a></span></p>

               <figure class="figure" id="advs595-fig-0010"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c8ae465f-7e54-43a9-b065-0bf700b4f18f/advs595-fig-0010-m.jpg"><picture>

            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs595-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/84b18ad6-f995-4d60-8082-c09658badfbf/advs595-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/84b18ad6-f995-4d60-8082-c09658badfbf/advs595-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dc02aa5b-bc68-4b99-9152-ee34732a4159/advs595-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/84b18ad6-f995-4d60-8082-c09658badfbf/advs595-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs595-fig-0011"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/38c13352-463b-47c4-a09b-4fe21de5d2fb/advs595-fig-0011-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/38c13352-463b-47c4-a09b-4fe21de5d2fb/advs595-fig-0011-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aff8dede-bd36-42f1-ab00-2b5c65327b0a/advs595-fig-0011-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/38c13352-463b-47c4-a09b-4fe21de5d2fb/advs595-fig-0011-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs595-fig-0011&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201800080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of 1D CNF in detail.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs595-sec-0040">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs595-sec-0040-title">2.2 The Growth of 1D CNF-400/500/600/700s</h3>
            
            <p>Owing to the fascinated transformation from 0D CQDs to 1D CNF, it is interesting to explore the effect of calcination temperatures (300, 400, 500, 600, and 700 °C) on its evolution process. The formation mechanism and microstructure were investigated through TEM, HRTEM, and SAED patterns, as shown in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0004">4</a>. Obviously, 0D CQDs were not pyrolyzed to carbon atoms, which were still dispersed uniformly in ethanol in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0004">4</a>A2. Interestingly, with the increased temperature, the higher catalytic activity was aroused by more thermal energy, leading to the fact that the shuttle structure of CNF is composed of thin primitive rods, which were wrapped more tightly in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0004">4</a>B1–E1. The growth mechanism of CNF is similar to that of rattans in nature, which all depends on the strong interaction force.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0033_R_d68468175e1135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Alpine Bot.">33</a></span> The mechanism was further demonstrated by the gradually closing cusps in the magnification TEM of the cusps in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0004">4</a>B2–E2. Moreover, no apparent long-range ordered structure is found in HRTEM images of CNFs, but some obvious turbostratic graphitic microstructures are observed, revealing the amorphous nature of the samples of hard carbon materials.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0034_R_d68468175e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Talanta Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a></span> With the increased carbonization temperatures, different ranges of structures constructed by the hexagonal layer of carbon and the layer-stacked-graphene sheets grow evidently, which is attributed to the fact that the orientated growth of carbon atoms was induced by ZnO. The increased disordered structures could supply more approaches for metal ions' insertion and plentiful surface defects, improving the adsorption ability.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0035_R_d68468175e1142" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Energy">35</a></span> In Figure 8S (Supporting Information), the broken root is clearly observed, and the disordered lattice shows the growth direction. This type of preferred orientation could also be formed in SAED images with the rising long axis of ellipse, which are indexing to the characteristic peaks of carbon (002), agreed well with the analysis of XRD. The sharpness of ellipse is not uniform in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0004">4</a>, perhaps resulted from the defects and lattice distortion. Moreover, no crystal diffraction spots appearing is another demonstration of amorphous carbon, and the improved degree of graphitization is further verified through the enhancing sharpness of diffraction rings.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0036_R_d68468175e1146" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Mater. Chem. A">36</a></span> According to the equation (<i>λL</i> = <i>Rd</i>), the similar <i>d</i> value was calculated with the assistance of cycle radius from SAED patterns, which is consistence with the TEM analysis.</p>
            
            <p>The SEM images of CNF-400, CNF-500, CNF-600, and CNF-700 were displayed in Figure 6S (Supporting Information). Figure 4S (Supporting Information) also presents the SEM images of CNF-300 without any rod-like structures, confirming that CNFs were not formed at low temperature. When the temperature increased to 400 °C, some ateliosis wires were wrapped into the shape of rods in Figure 6Sa,b (Supporting Information). Clearly, more formation energy was supplied at higher temperature to build the well-shuttle shape but with some branches at the cusp of the sample in Figure 6Sc,d (Supporting Information). From the images of CNF-600/700 in Figure 6Se–h (Supporting Information), the shuttle rods with a closed sharp corner were constructed completely and CNF-600 displays intact rod structure as compared to CNF-700, suggesting that CNF-700 was broken in the rapid formation process with highest energy. Moreover, in Figure 7S (Supporting Information), the available ratio of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> and the annealing times are vital for the formation of rod structure.</p>
            
            <p>The detailed lattice spaces, degree of graphite, functional groups, and special surface area for 1D structures were investigated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs595-fig-0005">5</a> and the corresponding fitting curves are shown in Figure 9S (Supporting Information). With improved carbonization temperature, the (002) peaks' positions were shifted toward a low angel (from 23.5<sup>o</sup> to 22.3<sup>o</sup>), which are calculated as the lattice expands gradually from 0.373 to 0.397 nm, facilitating the insertion–extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0037_R_d68468175e1175" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Energy">37</a></span> In Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#advs595-tbl-0001">1</a>, the value of <i>L<sub>c</sub></i>/<i>d</i><sub>(002)</sub> is found from 4.9 to 5.9, indicating the increased interlayer in the graphitic domain,<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0022_R_d68468175e1186" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Lett.">22</a></span> which agrees well with the analysis of TEM in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0004">4</a>. The Raman spectra of CNF-400/500/600/700 were displayed in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0005">5</a>B, showing the improved degree of graphite form the variation of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>, which is beneficial for the shuttling of sodium ions at high-rate current densities.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0038_R_d68468175e1199" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></span> Moreover, differing from the obtained carbon materials at high temperature, the peak at 1200 cm<sup>−1</sup> (C<span class="icomoon"></span>O) was found decreasing, suggesting the reduction of numerous oxygen-containing groups.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0039_R_d68468175e1204" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Supercrit. Fluids J. Electroanal. Chem.">39</a></span> Plentiful functional groups were further confirmed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs595-fig-0006">6</a>C, which are <span class="icomoon"></span>CH<sub>2</sub>, <span class="icomoon"></span>CH<sub>3</sub>, O<span class="icomoon"></span>C<span class="icomoon"></span>O, CO, and C<span class="icomoon"></span>OH, respectively. The special surface areas of the CNF-400/500/600/700 samples are 697.3, 841.2, 687.5, and 173.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, derived from the structure growth speeds for the carbonized temperature, which effectively demonstrated that the rods were intertwined more tightly.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs595-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/043c1026-d3bc-41dd-adb9-e579b2d24932/advs595-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/043c1026-d3bc-41dd-adb9-e579b2d24932/advs595-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1e23fcf3-356c-402e-b227-3ef7ae7b11e4/advs595-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/043c1026-d3bc-41dd-adb9-e579b2d24932/advs595-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs595-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201800080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A) XRD, B) Raman, C) FTIR, and D) specific surface area (Brunauer–Emmett–Teller; BET) of CNF-400/500/600/700.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs595-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/aafdcff1-ffa4-43d9-92af-a2bbf0bb8f87/advs595-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/aafdcff1-ffa4-43d9-92af-a2bbf0bb8f87/advs595-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d8533e44-a951-4811-9269-71a497dc7b47/advs595-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/aafdcff1-ffa4-43d9-92af-a2bbf0bb8f87/advs595-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs595-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201800080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A) The cycling performances, B) Coulombic efficiency, C,E) and rate properties of 0D CQDs, 1D CNF, 2D CNS, and 3D CFW. D) The discharge platforms of 1D CNF.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The growth mechanism of 1D CNF is proposed, in detail, in Scheme <a href="#advs595-fig-0011">2</a>. Note that 0D CQDs were not fully decomposed into the carbon and oxygen atoms, but rather agglomerated large organic particles at 300 °C. When the annealing temperature (AT) was increased to 400 °C, the formation of ZnO can successfully induce the formation of 1D structure. However, the weak catalytic activity prompts the generation of 1D CNF with very rough surface and insufficient energy, where the coiling cannot be triggered. With the improvement of AT to 500 °C, the activity of ZnO was further enhanced, facilitating carbon atoms gathering into the 1D structure, forming relatively smooth 1D CNF. Meanwhile, taking account of the unstable roots and more energy, the rods play an important role as the mutual support to maintain this grows upward, so the tips of rods have been wrapped to some extent. At high temperature (600 °C), the increased induction force accelerates the moving of carbon atoms toward the center of construction, leading to the reducing of scraps. The twine draws to an end, approximately forming the basiconic carbon structures. Eventually, the temperature (700 °C) makes the carbon atoms run up to the surface of 1D structure, which brings the relatively smooth rods with numerous defects. Note that the catalysts obtained from different temperatures have various activities.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0040_R_d68468175e1256" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Rare Metals">40</a></span> The wrapped process was accomplished to obtain the vine structure. In addition, the orientation of the crystalline lattice is boosted with the increased AT. In the nature, it is found that the formation mechanism of 1D structure is similar to that of vines,<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0033_R_d68468175e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Alpine Bot.">33</a></span> and all of them depend on the external force (magnetic force and induced force) and mutual supporting.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs595-sec-0050">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs595-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Properties</h3>
            
            <p>The discharge/discharge cycling performances of the as-derived samples are displayed in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0006">6</a>A at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). By comparison of 0/1/2/3D electrodes, it is clear that 1D CNF exhibits the best Na-storage stability in cycling, retaining a reversible capacity of 301.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. The discharge capacities were also kept 140 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for 0D CQDS (bulk materials), 128.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for 2D CNS, and 217.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for 3D CFW. In Figure 8S (Supporting Information), the charge–discharge platforms of different cycles were compared, revealing that the fundamental Na-storage mechanism has not changed. The capacity fades in the first 20 cycles for all samples, and the following cycles are stable, which is due to the irreversible reaction and the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0041_R_d68468175e1287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></span> The excellent electrochemical property for 1D CNF was concluded to two reasons: (1) plenty of defects on the surface are beneficial for sodium ions' adsorption/desorption; (2) long-term disordered graphitize structures with large layer distance provide much insertion entrances. Moreover, the high initial capacity and the low residual capacity of 2D CNS are observed, which is ascribed that large-scale graphenes are easy to stick together in cycling progress, resulting in the serious capacity fading. It is further demonstrated that 3D CFW with steady framework presents the stable Na-storage property. In addition, the Coulombic efficiency of 0/1/2/3D electrodes are ≈51%, 53%, 37%, and 21% in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0006">6</a>B and in Figure 10S (Supporting Information), which is attributed to the fact that the irreversible capacity is relative to the variation of special surface area, agreeing well with the previous report.<span><a href="#advs595-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs595-bib-0042_R_d68468175e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Power Sources Electrochim. Acta Nat. Energy">42</a></span> It is concluded that the special characteristics of 0/1/2/3D samples have significant effects on the cycling stability, which also affect their rate performances. In Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0006">6</a>C, the excellent fast Na-storage properties of 1D CNF is impressive, 309, 272, 221, 183, 145, 117, and 95 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.5, 5.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which is better than the majority of the previous works in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#advs595-tbl-0002">2</a>. Meanwhile, 2D CNS and 3D CFW also exhibit the satisfactory transference of sodium ions, delivering large reversible capacities of 118 and 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Thus, the platforms at stepwise current densities for 1D CNF are presented in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0006">6</a>D to investigate the surface-induced capacitive process (SCP) and diffusion-controlled intercalation process (DIP). The steep platform at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> is near to line, while the slopes of small current densities (e.g., 0.1 and 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>) are much more mild and arced, further revealing that the total capacity derives from two sources SCP and DIP at low current levels. As well known, the capacity and cycling properties at large current density seriously relied on the SCP behaviors. The rate capacities of 1/2/3D samples at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> are compared in Figure <a href="#advs595-fig-0006">6</a>E, displaying stable capacities of 107, 58, and 86 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (after 5000 cycles), respectively, which meet the commercial electrode materials. In Figure 11S (Supporting Information), the long-cycling properties of 1D CNF were displayed, and the capacities can be kept 217.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles, 201.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles, and 147.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 cycles, further verifying the excellent electrochemical properties of 1D CNF.</p>
